					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Data item stored in a browser by a website</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<table class="sidebar nomobile nowraplinks hlist" role="navigation"><tbody><tr><th class="sidebar-title"><a href="/wiki/HTTP" title="HTTP">HTTP</a></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-image"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:HTTP_logo.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/HTTP_logo.svg/250px-HTTP_logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="180" height="96" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/HTTP_logo.svg/330px-HTTP_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/HTTP_logo.svg/500px-HTTP_logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="274"></a></span></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">

</tr><tr><th class="sidebar-heading">

<li><a href="/wiki/Digest_access_authentication" title="Digest access authentication">Digest access authentication</a></li></ul></td>
</tr><tr><th class="sidebar-heading">
Security vulnerabilities</th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/HTTP_header_injection" title="HTTP header injection">HTTP header injection</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/HTTP_request_smuggling" title="HTTP request smuggling">HTTP request smuggling</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/HTTP_response_splitting" title="HTTP response splitting">HTTP response splitting</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/HTTP_parameter_pollution" title="HTTP parameter pollution">HTTP parameter pollution</a></li></ul></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:HTTP" title="Template:HTTP"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:HTTP" title="Template talk:HTTP"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:HTTP" title="Special:EditPage/Template:HTTP"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>An <b>HTTP cookie</b> (also called <b>web cookie</b>, <b>Internet cookie</b>, <b>browser cookie</b>, or simply <b>cookie</b>) is a small block of <a href="/wiki/Data_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Data (computing)">data</a> created by a <a href="/wiki/Web_server" title="Web server">web server</a> while a <a href="/wiki/User_(computing)" title="User (computing)">user</a> is <a href="/wiki/Browsing" title="Browsing">browsing</a> a <a href="/wiki/Website" title="Website">website</a> and placed on the user's computer or other device by the user's <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">web browser</a>. Cookies are placed on the device used to access a website, and more than one cookie may be placed on a user's device during a session.
</p><p>Cookies serve useful and sometimes essential functions on the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">web</a>. They enable web servers to store <a href="/wiki/Program_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Program state">stateful</a> information (such as items added in the shopping cart in an <a href="/wiki/Online_shopping" title="Online shopping">online store</a>) on the user's device or to track the user's browsing activity (including clicking particular buttons, <a href="/wiki/Access_control" title="Access control">logging in</a>, or recording which <a href="/wiki/Web_browsing_history" title="Web browsing history">pages were visited in the past</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They can also be used to save information that the user previously entered into <a href="/wiki/Form_(HTML)" class="mw-redirect" title="Form (HTML)">form fields</a>, such as names, addresses, <a href="/wiki/Password" title="Password">passwords</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Payment_card_number" title="Payment card number">payment card numbers</a> for subsequent use.
</p><p><b>Authentication cookies</b> are commonly used by web servers to <a href="/wiki/Authentication" title="Authentication">authenticate</a> that a user is logged in, and with which <a href="/wiki/Account_verification" title="Account verification">account</a> they are logged in. Without the cookie, users would need to authenticate themselves by logging in on each page containing sensitive information that they wish to access. The security of an authentication cookie generally depends on the security of the issuing website and the user's web browser, and on whether the cookie data is <a href="/wiki/Encryption" title="Encryption">encrypted</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vulnerability (computing)">Security vulnerabilities</a> may allow a cookie's data to be read by an <a href="/wiki/Security_hacker" title="Security hacker">attacker</a>, used to gain access to <a href="/wiki/Personal_data" title="Personal data">user data</a>, or used to gain access (with the user's credentials) to the website to which the cookie belongs (see <a href="/wiki/Cross-site_scripting" title="Cross-site scripting">cross-site scripting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cross-site_request_forgery" title="Cross-site request forgery">cross-site request forgery</a> for examples).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-UjTred&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UjTred-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><b>Tracking cookies</b>, and especially <a href="#Third-party_cookie">third-party tracking cookies</a>, are commonly used as ways to compile long-term records of individuals' <a href="/wiki/Web_browsing_history" title="Web browsing history">browsing histories</a> — a potential <a href="/wiki/Internet_privacy#HTTP_cookies" title="Internet privacy">privacy concern</a> that prompted European<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mb6wv&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mb6wv-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and U.S. lawmakers to take action in 2011.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-eulaw&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eulaw-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-KM1a4&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KM1a4-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> European law requires that all websites targeting <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> member states gain "<a href="/wiki/Informed_consent" title="Informed consent">informed consent</a>" from users before storing non-essential cookies on their device.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Background">Background</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Origin_of_the_name">Origin of the name</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cookie_stack.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Cookie_stack.jpg/250px-Cookie_stack.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Cookie_stack.jpg/500px-Cookie_stack.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000"></a><figcaption>HTTP cookies share their name with <a href="/wiki/Cookie" title="Cookie">a popular baked treat</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The term <i>cookie</i> was coined by web-browser programmer <a href="/wiki/Lou_Montulli" title="Lou Montulli">Lou Montulli</a>. It was derived from the term <i><a href="/wiki/Magic_cookie" title="Magic cookie">magic cookie</a></i>, which is a packet of data a program receives and sends back unchanged, used by <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">Unix</a> programmers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wdi6I&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wdi6I-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-xVtjM&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xVtjM-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="History">History</h3></div>
<p>Magic cookies were already used in computing when computer programmer <a href="/wiki/Lou_Montulli" title="Lou Montulli">Lou Montulli</a> had the idea of using them in web communications in June 1994.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-N4WV7&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-N4WV7-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At the time, he was an employee of <a href="/wiki/Netscape_Communications" class="mw-redirect" title="Netscape Communications">Netscape Communications</a>, which was developing an <a href="/wiki/E-commerce" title="E-commerce">e-commerce</a> application for <a href="/wiki/MCI_Inc." title="MCI Inc.">MCI</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vint_Cerf" title="Vint Cerf">Vint Cerf</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Klensin" title="John Klensin">John Klensin</a> represented MCI in technical discussions with Netscape Communications. MCI did not want its servers to have to retain partial transaction states, which led them to ask Netscape to find a way to store that state in each user's computer instead. Cookies provided a solution to the problem of reliably implementing a <a href="/wiki/Shopping_cart_software" title="Shopping cart software">virtual shopping cart</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-kesan&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kesan-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-kristol&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kristol-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Together with John Giannandrea, Montulli wrote the initial Netscape cookie specification the same year. Version 0.9beta of <a href="/wiki/Netscape_Navigator" title="Netscape Navigator">Mosaic Netscape</a>, released on 13 October 1994,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-JgNeY&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JgNeY-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-8YpTv&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8YpTv-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> supported cookies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-kristol&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kristol-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The first use of cookies (out of the labs) was checking whether visitors to the Netscape website had already visited the site. Montulli applied for a patent for the cookie technology in 1995, which was granted in 1998.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Support for cookies was integrated with <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer" title="Internet Explorer">Internet Explorer</a> in version 2, released in October 1995.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-95BiI&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95BiI-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The introduction of cookies was not widely known to the public at the time. In particular, cookies were accepted by default, and users were not notified of their presence.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The public learned about cookies after the <i><a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a></i> published an article about them on 12 February 1996.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-B3JMd&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B3JMd-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the same year, cookies received a lot of media attention, especially because of potential privacy implications. Cookies were discussed in two U.S. <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a> hearings in 1996 and 1997.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-UjTred&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UjTred-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The development of the formal cookie specifications was already ongoing. In particular, the first discussions about a formal specification started in April 1995 on the www-talk <a href="/wiki/Electronic_mailing_list" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic mailing list">mailing list</a>. A special working group within the <a href="/wiki/Internet_Engineering_Task_Force" title="Internet Engineering Task Force">Internet Engineering Task Force</a> (IETF) was formed. Two alternative proposals for introducing state in HTTP transactions had been proposed by <a href="/wiki/Brian_Behlendorf" title="Brian Behlendorf">Brian Behlendorf</a> and David Kristol respectively. But the group, headed by Kristol himself and Lou Montulli, soon decided to use the Netscape specification as a starting point. In February 1996, the working group identified third-party cookies as a considerable privacy threat. The specification produced by the group was eventually published as RFC 2109 in February 1997. It specifies that third-party cookies were either not allowed at all, or at least not enabled by default.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-&#82;FC2109&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFC2109-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At this time, advertising companies were already using third-party cookies. The recommendation about third-party cookies of RFC 2109 was not followed by Netscape and Internet Explorer. RFC 2109 was superseded by RFC 2965 in October 2000.
</p><p>RFC 2965 added a <code>Set-Cookie2</code> <a href="/wiki/HTTP_header" class="mw-redirect" title="HTTP header">header field</a>, which informally came to be called "RFC 2965-style cookies" as opposed to the original <code>Set-Cookie</code> header field which was called "Netscape-style cookies".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AGgFj&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AGgFj-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-V1mES&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-V1mES-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <code>Set-Cookie2</code> was seldom used, however, and was <a href="/wiki/Deprecate" class="mw-redirect" title="Deprecate">deprecated</a> in RFC 6265 in April 2011 which was written as a definitive specification for cookies as used in the real world.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-StateMgmt&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StateMgmt-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> No modern browser recognizes the <code>Set-Cookie2</code> header field.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-TASE4&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TASE4-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Terminology">Terminology</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Session_cookie">Session cookie</h3></div>
<p>A <i>session cookie</i> (also known as an <i>in-memory cookie</i>, <i>transient cookie</i> or <i>non-persistent cookie</i>) exists only in temporary memory while the user navigates a website.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mscookie&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mscookie-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Session cookies expire or are deleted when the user closes the web browser.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-HwxT6&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HwxT6-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Session cookies are identified by the browser by the absence of an expiration date assigned to them.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Persistent_cookie">Persistent cookie</h3></div>
<p>A <i>persistent cookie</i> expires at a specific date or after a specific length of time. For the persistent cookie's lifespan set by its creator, its information will be transmitted to the server every time the user visits the website that it belongs to, or every time the user views a resource belonging to that website from another website (such as an advertisement).
</p><p>For this reason, persistent cookies are sometimes referred to as <i>tracking cookies</i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> because they can be used by advertisers to record information about a user's web browsing habits over an extended period of time. Persistent cookies are also used for reasons such as keeping users logged into their accounts on websites, to avoid re-entering login credentials at every visit. <span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable crossreference selfref">(See <a href="#Uses">§&#160;Uses</a>, below.)</span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Secure_cookie">Secure cookie</h3></div>
<p>A <i>secure cookie</i> can only be transmitted over an encrypted connection (i.e. <a href="/wiki/HTTP_Secure" class="mw-redirect" title="HTTP Secure">HTTPS</a>). They cannot be transmitted over unencrypted connections (i.e. <a href="/wiki/HTTP" title="HTTP">HTTP</a>). This makes the cookie less likely to be exposed to cookie theft via <a href="/wiki/Network_eavesdropping" title="Network eavesdropping">eavesdropping</a>. A cookie is made secure by adding the <code>Secure</code> flag to the cookie.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Http-only_cookie"><span class="anchor" id="HttpOnly&#95;cookie"></span>Http-only cookie</h3></div>
<p>An <i>http-only cookie</i> cannot be accessed by client-side APIs, such as <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a>. This restriction eliminates the threat of cookie theft via <a href="/wiki/Cross-site_scripting" title="Cross-site scripting">cross-site scripting</a> (XSS).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the cookie remains vulnerable to <a href="/wiki/Cross-site_tracing" title="Cross-site tracing">cross-site tracing</a> (XST) and <a href="/wiki/Cross-site_request_forgery" title="Cross-site request forgery">cross-site request forgery</a> (CSRF) attacks. A cookie is given this characteristic by adding the <code>HttpOnly</code> flag to the cookie.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Same-site_cookie"><span class="anchor" id="SameSite&#95;cookie"></span>Same-site cookie</h3></div>
<p>In 2016 <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a> version 51 introduced<sup id="cite&#95;ref-T8177&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T8177-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> a new kind of cookie with attribute <code>SameSite</code> with possible values of <code>Strict</code>, <code>Lax</code> or <code>None</code>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-oCqyo&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oCqyo-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With attribute <code>SameSite=Strict</code>, the browsers would only send cookies to a target domain that is the same as the origin domain. This would effectively mitigate <a href="/wiki/Cross-site_request_forgery" title="Cross-site request forgery">cross-site request forgery</a> (CSRF) attacks. With <code>SameSite=Lax</code>, browsers would send cookies with requests to a target domain even it is different from the origin domain, but only for <i>safe</i> requests such as GET (POST is unsafe) and not third-party cookies (inside iframe). Attribute <code>SameSite=None</code> would allow third-party (cross-site) cookies, however, most browsers require <a href="#Secure_cookie">secure attribute</a> on SameSite=None cookies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-vQf6D&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vQf6D-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The Same-site cookie is incorporated into a new RFC draft for "Cookies: HTTP State Management Mechanism"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to update RFC 6265 (if approved).
</p><p>Chrome, Firefox, and Edge started to support Same-site cookies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sJzIz&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sJzIz-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The key of rollout is the treatment of existing cookies without the SameSite attribute defined, Chrome has been treating those existing cookies as if SameSite=None, this would let all website/applications run as before. Google intended to change that default to <code>SameSite=Lax</code> in Chrome 80 planned to be released in February 2020,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-QPJhf&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QPJhf-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but due to potential for breakage of those applications/websites that rely on third-party/cross-site cookies and <a href="/wiki/COVID-19" title="COVID-19">COVID-19</a> circumstances, Google postponed this change to Chrome 84.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ne4hV&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ne4hV-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Supercookie">Supercookie</h3></div>
<p>A <i>supercookie</i> is a cookie with an origin of a <a href="/wiki/Top-level_domain" title="Top-level domain">top-level domain</a> (such as <code>.com</code>) or a public suffix (such as <code>.co.uk</code>). Ordinary cookies, by contrast, have an origin of a specific domain name, such as <code>example.com</code>.
</p><p>Supercookies can be a potential security concern and are therefore often blocked by web browsers. If unblocked by the browser, an attacker in control of a malicious website could set a supercookie and potentially disrupt or impersonate legitimate user requests to another website that shares the same top-level domain or public suffix as the malicious website. For example, a supercookie with an origin of <code>.com</code> could maliciously affect a request made to <code>example.com</code>, even if the cookie did not originate from <code>example.com</code>. This can be used to fake logins or change user information.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Public_Suffix_List" title="Public Suffix List">Public Suffix List</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-oN0iG&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oN0iG-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> helps to mitigate the risk that supercookies pose. The Public Suffix List is a cross-vendor initiative that aims to provide an accurate and up-to-date list of domain name suffixes. Older versions of browsers may not have an up-to-date list, and will therefore be vulnerable to supercookies from certain domains.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Other_uses">Other uses</h4></div>
<p>The term <i>supercookie</i> is also used for tracking technologies that do not rely on HTTP cookies. Two such <i>supercookie</i> mechanisms were found on Microsoft websites in August 2011: <a href="/wiki/Cookie_syncing" title="Cookie syncing">cookie syncing</a> that respawned MUID (machine unique identifier) cookies, and <a href="/wiki/HTTP_ETag" title="HTTP ETag">ETag</a> cookies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ivyb7&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivyb7-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Due to media attention, Microsoft later disabled this code.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8LKGy&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8LKGy-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In a 2021 blog post, Mozilla used the term <i>supercookie</i> to refer to <a href="#Browser_cache">the use of browser cache</a> as a means of tracking users across sites.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-PyGKV&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PyGKV-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Zombie_cookie">Zombie cookie</h3></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Zombie_cookie" title="Zombie cookie">Zombie cookie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evercookie" title="Evercookie">Evercookie</a></div>
<p>A <i>zombie cookie</i> is data and code that has been placed by a <a href="/wiki/Web_server" title="Web server">web server</a> on a visitor's computer or other device in a hidden location outside the visitor's <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">web browser</a>'s dedicated cookie storage location, and that automatically recreates a HTTP cookie as a regular cookie after the original cookie had been deleted. The zombie cookie may be stored in multiple locations, such as <a href="/wiki/Local_shared_object" title="Local shared object">Flash Local shared object</a>, <a href="/wiki/Web_storage" title="Web storage">HTML5 Web storage</a>, and other client-side and even server-side locations, and when absence is detected in one of the locations, the missing instance is recreated by the JavaScript code using the data stored in other locations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MLbxD&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLbxD-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-k81iO&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k81iO-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Structure">Structure</h2></div>
<p>A cookie consists of the following components:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Peng,&#95;Weihong&#95;2000&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peng,_Weihong_2000-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Stenberg,&#95;Daniel&#95;2009&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stenberg,_Daniel_2009-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Name</li>
<li>Value</li>
<li>Zero or more attributes (<a href="/wiki/Attribute%E2%80%93value_pair" class="mw-redirect" title="Attribute–value pair">name/value pairs</a>). Attributes store information such as the cookie's expiration, domain, and flags (such as <code>Secure</code> and <code>HttpOnly</code>).</li></ol>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Uses">Uses</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Session_management">Session management</h3></div>
<p>Cookies were originally introduced to provide a way for users to record items they want to purchase as they navigate throughout a website (a virtual <i>shopping cart</i> or <i>shopping basket</i>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-kesan&#95;9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kesan-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-kristol&#95;10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kristol-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Today, however, the contents of a user's shopping cart are usually stored in a database on the server, rather than in a cookie on the client. To keep track of which user is assigned to which shopping cart, the server sends a cookie to the client that contains a <a href="/wiki/Unique_identifier" title="Unique identifier">unique session identifier</a> (typically, a long string of random letters and numbers). Because cookies are sent to the server with every request the client makes, that session identifier will be sent back to the server every time the user visits a new page on the website, which lets the server know which shopping cart to display to the user.
</p><p>Another popular use of cookies is for logging into websites. When the user visits a website's login page, the web server typically sends the client a cookie containing a unique session identifier. When the user successfully logs in, the server remembers that that particular session identifier has been authenticated and grants the user access to its services.
</p><p>Because session cookies only contain a unique session identifier, this makes the amount of personal information that a website can save about each user virtually limitless—the website is not limited to restrictions concerning how large a cookie can be. Session cookies also help to improve page load times, since the amount of information in a session cookie is small and requires little bandwidth.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Personalization">Personalization</h3></div>
<p>Cookies can be used to remember information about the user in order to show relevant content to that user over time. For example, a web server might send a cookie containing the username that was last used to log into a website, so that it may be filled in automatically the next time the user logs in.
</p><p>Many websites use cookies for personalization based on the user's preferences. Users select their preferences by entering them in a web form and submitting the form to the server. The server encodes the preferences in a cookie and sends the cookie back to the browser. This way, every time the user accesses a page on the website, the server can personalize the page according to the user's preferences. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> search engine once used cookies to allow users (even non-registered ones) to decide how many search results per page they wanted to see.
Also, <a href="/wiki/DuckDuckGo" title="DuckDuckGo">DuckDuckGo</a> uses cookies to allow users to set the viewing preferences like colors of the web page.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Tracking">Tracking</h3></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Web_tracking" title="Web tracking">Web tracking</a></div>
<p>Tracking cookies are used to track users' web browsing habits. This can also be done to some extent by using the <a href="/wiki/IP_address" title="IP address">IP address</a> of the computer requesting the page or the <a href="/wiki/HTTP_referer" title="HTTP referer">referer</a> field of the <a href="/wiki/HTTP" title="HTTP">HTTP</a> request header, but cookies allow for greater precision. This can be demonstrated as follows:
</p>
<ol><li>If the user requests a page of the site, but the request contains no cookie, the server presumes that this is the first page visited by the user. So the server creates a unique identifier (typically a string of random letters and numbers) and sends it as a cookie back to the browser together with the requested page.</li>
<li>From this point on, the cookie will automatically be sent by the browser to the server every time a new page from the site is requested. The server not only sends the page as usual but also stores the URL of the requested page, the date/time of the request, and the cookie in a log file.</li></ol>
<p>By analyzing this log file, it is then possible to find out which pages the user has visited, in what sequence, and for how long.
</p><p>Corporations exploit users' web habits by tracking cookies to collect information about buying habits. The <i><a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Journal" class="mw-redirect" title="Wall Street Journal">Wall Street Journal</a></i> found that America's top fifty websites installed an average of sixty-four pieces of tracking technology onto computers, resulting in a total of 3,180 tracking files.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-tpktn&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tpktn-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The data can then be collected and sold to bidding corporations.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Implementation">Implementation</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:HTTP_cookie_exchange.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/HTTP_cookie_exchange.svg/250px-HTTP_cookie_exchange.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="139" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/HTTP_cookie_exchange.svg/500px-HTTP_cookie_exchange.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="390"></a><figcaption>A possible interaction between a web browser and a web server holding a web page in which the server sends a cookie to the browser and the browser sends it back when requesting another page</figcaption></figure>
<p>Cookies are arbitrary pieces of data, usually chosen and first sent by the web server, and stored on the client computer by the web browser. The browser then sends them back to the server with every request, introducing <a href="/wiki/State_(computer_science)" title="State (computer science)">states</a> (memory of previous events) into otherwise stateless <a href="/wiki/HTTP" title="HTTP">HTTP</a> transactions. Without cookies, each retrieval of a <a href="/wiki/Web_page" title="Web page">web page</a> or component of a web page would be an isolated event, largely unrelated to all other page views made by the user on the website. Although cookies are usually set by the web server, they can also be set by the client using a scripting language such as <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a> (unless the cookie's <code>HttpOnly</code> flag is set, in which case the cookie cannot be modified by scripting languages).
</p><p>The cookie specifications<sup id="cite&#95;ref-httponlyrfc&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-httponlyrfc-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-NWRaX&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NWRaX-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> require that browsers meet the following requirements in order to support cookies:
</p>
<ul><li>Can support cookies as large as 4,096 <a href="/wiki/Byte" title="Byte">bytes</a> in size.</li>
<li>Can support at least 50 cookies per <a href="/wiki/Internet_domain" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet domain">domain</a> (i.e. per website).</li>
<li>Can support at least 3,000 cookies in total.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Setting_a_cookie">Setting a cookie</h3></div>
<p>Cookies are set using the <code>Set-Cookie</code> <a href="/wiki/HTTP_header" class="mw-redirect" title="HTTP header">header field</a>, sent in an HTTP response from the web server. This header field instructs the web browser to store the cookie and send it back in future requests to the server (the browser will ignore this header field if it does not support cookies or has disabled cookies).
</p><p>As an example, the browser sends its first HTTP request for the homepage of the <code>www.example.org</code> website:
</p>
<div class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-http mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr"><pre><span></span><span class="nf">GET</span> <span class="nn">/index.html</span> <span class="kr">HTTP</span><span class="o">/</span><span class="m">1.1</span>
<span class="na">Host</span><span class="o">:</span> <span class="l">www.example.org</span>
<span class="err">...</span>
</pre></div>
<p>The server responds with two <code>Set-Cookie</code> header fields:
</p>
<div class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-http mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr"><pre><span></span><span class="kr">HTTP</span><span class="o">/</span><span class="m">1.0</span> <span class="m">200</span> <span class="ne">OK</span>
<span class="na">Content-type</span><span class="o">:</span> <span class="l">text/html</span>
<span class="na">Set-Cookie</span><span class="o">:</span> <span class="l">theme=light</span>
<span class="na">Set-Cookie</span><span class="o">:</span> <span class="l">sessionToken=abc123; Expires=Wed, 9 Jun 2021 10:18:14 GMT</span>
<span class="err">...</span>
</pre></div>
<p>The server's HTTP response contains the contents of the website's homepage. But it also instructs the browser to set two cookies. The first, <i>theme</i>, is considered to be a <i>session cookie</i> since it does not have an <code>Expires</code> or <code>Max-Age</code> attribute. Session cookies are intended to be deleted by the browser when the browser closes. The second, <i>sessionToken</i>, is considered to be a <i>persistent cookie</i> since it contains an <code>Expires</code> attribute, which instructs the browser to delete the cookie at a specific date and time.
</p><p>Next, the browser sends another request to visit the <code>spec.html</code> page on the website. This request contains a <code>Cookie</code> header field, which contains the two cookies that the server instructed the browser to set:
</p>
<div class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-http mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr"><pre><span></span><span class="nf">GET</span> <span class="nn">/spec.html</span> <span class="kr">HTTP</span><span class="o">/</span><span class="m">1.1</span>
<span class="na">Host</span><span class="o">:</span> <span class="l">www.example.org</span>
<span class="na">Cookie</span><span class="o">:</span> <span class="l">theme=light; sessionToken=abc123</span>
<span class="err">...</span>
</pre></div>
<p>This way, the server knows that this HTTP request is related to the previous one. The server would answer by sending the requested page, possibly including more <code>Set-Cookie</code> header fields in the HTTP response in order to instruct the browser to add new cookies, modify existing cookies, or remove existing cookies. To remove a cookie, the server must include a <code>Set-Cookie</code> header field with an expiration date in the past.
</p><p>The value of a cookie may consist of any printable <a href="/wiki/ASCII" title="ASCII">ASCII</a> character (<code>!</code> through <code>~</code>, <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> <code>\u0021</code> through <code>\u007E</code>) excluding <code>,</code>and<code>;</code> and <a href="/wiki/Whitespace_character" title="Whitespace character">whitespace characters</a>. The name of a cookie excludes the same characters, as well as <code>=</code>, since that is the delimiter between the name and value. The cookie standard RFC 2965 is more restrictive but not implemented by browsers.
</p><p>The term <i>cookie crumb</i> is sometimes used to refer to a cookie's name–value pair.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CrumbExample&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrumbExample-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Cookies can also be set by scripting languages such as <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a> that run within the browser. In JavaScript, the object <code>document.cookie</code> is used for this purpose. For example, the instruction <code>document.cookie = "temperature=20"</code> creates a cookie of name <i>temperature</i> and value <i>20</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Q2GAk&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Q2GAk-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cookie_attributes">Cookie attributes</h3></div>
<p>In addition to a name and value, cookies can also have one or more attributes. Browsers do not include cookie attributes in requests to the server—they only send the cookie's name and value. Cookie attributes are used by browsers to determine when to delete a cookie, block a cookie or whether to send a cookie to the server.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Domain_and_Path">Domain and Path</h4></div>
<p>The <code>Domain</code> and <code>Path</code> attributes define the scope of the cookie. They essentially tell the browser what website the cookie belongs to. For security reasons, cookies can only be set on the current resource's top domain and its subdomains, and not for another domain and its subdomains. For example, the website <code>example.org</code> cannot set a cookie that has a domain of <code>foo.com</code> because this would allow the website <code>example.org</code> to control the cookies of the domain <code>foo.com</code>.
</p><p>If a cookie's <code>Domain</code> and <code>Path</code> attributes are not specified by the server, they default to the domain and path of the resource that was requested.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-uMnRY&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uMnRY-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, in most browsers there is a difference between a cookie set from <code>foo.com</code> without a domain, and a cookie set with the <code>foo.com</code> domain. In the former case, the cookie will only be sent for requests to <code>foo.com</code>, also known as a host-only cookie. In the latter case, all subdomains are also included (for example, <code>docs.foo.com</code>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-iDIGa&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iDIGa-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-5Qixt&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5Qixt-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A notable exception to this general rule is Edge prior to Windows 10 RS3 and Internet Explorer prior to IE 11 and Windows 10 RS4 (April 2018), which always sends cookies to subdomains regardless of whether the cookie was set with or without a domain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ry6VV&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ry6VV-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Below is an example of some <code>Set-Cookie</code> header fields in the HTTP response of a website after a user logged in. The HTTP request was sent to a webpage within the <code>docs.foo.com</code> subdomain:
</p>
<div class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-http mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr"><pre><span></span><span class="kr">HTTP</span><span class="o">/</span><span class="m">1.0</span> <span class="m">200</span> <span class="ne">OK</span>
<span class="na">Set-Cookie</span><span class="o">:</span> <span class="l">LSID=DQAAAK…Eaem_vYg; Path=/accounts; Expires=Wed, 13 Jan 2021 22:23:01 GMT; Secure; HttpOnly</span>
<span class="na">Set-Cookie</span><span class="o">:</span> <span class="l">HSID=AYQEVn…DKrdst; Domain=.foo.com; Path=/; Expires=Wed, 13 Jan 2021 22:23:01 GMT; HttpOnly</span>
<span class="na">Set-Cookie</span><span class="o">:</span> <span class="l">SSID=Ap4P…GTEq; Domain=foo.com; Path=/; Expires=Wed, 13 Jan 2021 22:23:01 GMT; Secure; HttpOnly</span>
<span class="err">...</span>
</pre></div>
<p>The first cookie, <code>LSID</code>, has no <code>Domain</code> attribute, and has a <code>Path</code> attribute set to <code>/accounts</code>. This tells the browser to use the cookie only when requesting pages contained in <code>docs.foo.com/accounts</code> (the domain is derived from the request domain). The other two cookies, <code>HSID</code> and <code>SSID</code>, would be used when the browser requests any subdomain in <code>.foo.com</code> on any path (for example <code>www.foo.com/bar</code>). The prepending dot is optional in recent standards, but can be added for compatibility with RFC 2109 based implementations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-gxcF2&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gxcF2-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Expires_and_Max-Age">Expires and Max-Age</h4></div>
<p>The <code>Expires</code> attribute defines a specific date and time for when the browser should delete the cookie. The date and time are specified in the form <code>Wdy, DD Mon YYYY HH:MM:SS GMT</code>, or in the form <code>Wdy, DD Mon YY HH:MM:SS GMT</code> for values of YY where YY is greater than or equal to 0 and less than or equal to 69.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-QKuwv&#95;55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QKuwv-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Alternatively, the <code>Max-Age</code> attribute can be used to set the cookie's expiration as an interval of seconds in the future, relative to the time the browser received the cookie. Below is an example of three <code>Set-Cookie</code> header fields that were received from a website after a user logged in:
</p>
<div class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-http mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr"><pre><span></span><span class="kr">HTTP</span><span class="o">/</span><span class="m">1.0</span> <span class="m">200</span> <span class="ne">OK</span>
<span class="na">Set-Cookie</span><span class="o">:</span> <span class="l">lu=Rg3vHJZnehYLjVg7qi3bZjzg; Expires=Tue, 15 Jan 2013 21:47:38 GMT; Path=/; Domain=.example.com; HttpOnly</span>
<span class="na">Set-Cookie</span><span class="o">:</span> <span class="l">made_write_conn=1295214458; Path=/; Domain=.example.com</span>
<span class="na">Set-Cookie</span><span class="o">:</span> <span class="l">reg_fb_gate=deleted; Expires=Thu, 1 Jan 1970 00:00:01 GMT; Path=/; Domain=.example.com; HttpOnly</span>
</pre></div>
<p>The first cookie, <code>lu</code>, is set to expire sometime on 15 January 2013. It will be used by the client browser until that time. The second cookie, <code>made_write_conn</code>, does not have an expiration date, making it a session cookie. It will be deleted after the user closes their browser. The third cookie, <code>reg_fb_gate</code>, has its value changed to <i>deleted</i>, with an expiration time in the past. The browser will delete this cookie right away because its expiration time is in the past. Note that cookie will only be deleted if the domain and path attributes in the <code>Set-Cookie</code> field match the values used when the cookie was created.
</p><p>As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HTTP_cookie&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> Internet Explorer did not support <code>Max-Age</code>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-nyQ00&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyQ00-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-wASD0&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wASD0-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Secure_and_HttpOnly">Secure and HttpOnly</h4></div>
<p>The <code>Secure</code> and <code>HttpOnly</code> attributes do not have associated values. Rather, the presence of just their attribute names indicates that their behaviors should be enabled.
</p><p>The <code>Secure</code> attribute is meant to keep cookie communication limited to encrypted transmission, directing browsers to use cookies only via <a href="/wiki/HTTPS" title="HTTPS">secure/encrypted</a> connections. However, if a web server sets a cookie with a secure attribute from a non-secure connection, the cookie can still be intercepted when it is sent to the user by <a href="/wiki/Man-in-the-middle_attack" title="Man-in-the-middle attack">man-in-the-middle attacks</a>. Therefore, for maximum security, cookies with the Secure attribute should only be set over a secure connection.
</p><p>The <code>HttpOnly</code> attribute directs browsers not to expose cookies through channels other than HTTP (and HTTPS) requests. This means that the cookie cannot be accessed via client-side scripting languages (notably <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a>), and therefore cannot be stolen easily via <a href="/wiki/Cross-site_scripting" title="Cross-site scripting">cross-site scripting</a> (a pervasive attack technique).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Symantec-2007-2nd-exec&#95;58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Symantec-2007-2nd-exec-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Browser_settings">Browser settings</h2></div>
<p>Most modern browsers support cookies and allow the user to disable them. The following are common options:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-eyMbr&#95;59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eyMbr-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>To enable or disable cookies completely, so that they are always accepted or always blocked.</li>
<li>To view and selectively delete cookies using a cookie manager.</li>
<li>To fully wipe all private data, including cookies.</li></ul>
<p>Add-on tools for managing cookie permissions also exist.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-microsoft2007&#95;60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-microsoft2007-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-bv35c&#95;61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bv35c-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-zuzB8&#95;62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zuzB8-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-xGdEB&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xGdEB-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Third-party_cookie"><span class="anchor" id="ThirdParty&#95;cookie"></span>Third-party cookie</h2></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Third-party_cookies" title="Third-party cookies">Third-party cookies</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Web_analytics#Problems_with_cookies" title="Web analytics">Web analytics §&#160;Problems with cookies</a></div>
<p>Cookies have some important implications for the privacy and anonymity of web users. While cookies are sent only to the server setting them or a server in the same Internet domain, a web page may contain images or other components stored on servers in other domains. Cookies that are set during retrieval of these components are called <i>third-party cookies</i>. A third-party cookie, belongs to a domain different from the one shown in the address bar. This sort of cookie typically appears when web pages feature content from external websites, such as <a href="/wiki/Banner_advertisement" class="mw-redirect" title="Banner advertisement">banner advertisements</a>. This opens up the potential for <a href="/wiki/Web_tracking" title="Web tracking">tracking</a> the user's browsing history and is used by advertisers to <a href="/wiki/Ad_server" class="mw-redirect" title="Ad server">serve relevant advertisements</a> to each user.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Third_party_cookie.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Third_party_cookie.png/250px-Third_party_cookie.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="173" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Third_party_cookie.png/500px-Third_party_cookie.png 1.5x" data-file-width="2460" data-file-height="1700"></a><figcaption>In this fictional example, an advertising company has placed banners in two websites. By hosting the banner images on its servers and using third-party cookies, the advertising company is able to track the browsing of users across these two sites.</figcaption></figure>
<p>As an example, suppose a user visits <code>www.example.org</code>. This website contains an advertisement from <code>ad.foxytracking.com</code>, which, when downloaded, sets a cookie belonging to the advertisement's domain (<code>ad.foxytracking.com</code>). Then, the user visits another website, <code>www.foo.com</code>, which also contains an advertisement from <code>ad.foxytracking.com</code> and sets a cookie belonging to that domain (<code>ad.foxytracking.com</code>). Eventually, both of these cookies will be sent to the advertiser when loading their advertisements or visiting their website. The advertiser can then use these cookies to build up a browsing history of the user across all the websites that have ads from this advertiser, through the use of the <a href="/wiki/HTTP_referer" title="HTTP referer">HTTP referer</a> header field.
</p><p>As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HTTP_cookie&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, some websites were setting cookies readable for over 100 third-party domains.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-BYMXD&#95;64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BYMXD-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On average, a single website was setting 10 cookies, with a maximum number of cookies (first- and third-party) reaching over 800.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-cOnAw&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cOnAw-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The older standards for cookies, RFC 2109<sup id="cite&#95;ref-&#82;FC2109&#95;17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFC2109-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and RFC 2965, recommend that browsers should protect user privacy and not allow sharing of cookies between servers by default. However, the newer standard, RFC 6265, explicitly allows user agents to implement whichever third-party cookie policy they wish. Most modern web browsers contain <a href="/wiki/Privacy_settings" title="Privacy settings">privacy settings</a> that can <a href="/wiki/Ad_blocker" class="mw-redirect" title="Ad blocker">block</a> third-party cookies. Since 2020, <a href="/wiki/Apple_Safari" class="mw-redirect" title="Apple Safari">Apple Safari</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-zw6bb&#95;66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zw6bb-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Firefox" title="Firefox">Firefox</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-GSofz&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GSofz-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Brave_(web_browser)" title="Brave (web browser)">Brave</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-sUPt1&#95;68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sUPt1-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> block all third-party cookies by default. Safari allows embedded sites to use Storage Access API to request permission to set first-party cookies. In May 2020, <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a> 83 introduced new features to block third-party cookies by default in its Incognito mode for private browsing, making blocking optional during normal browsing. The same update also added an option to block first-party cookies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-xiHRq&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xiHRq-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In April 2024, Chrome postponed third-party cookie blocking by default to 2025.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In July 2024, Google announced plan to avoid blocking third-party cookies by default and instead prompt users to allow third-party cookies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Privacy">Privacy</h2></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Do_Not_Track" title="Do Not Track">Do Not Track</a></div>
<p>The possibility of building a profile of users is a privacy threat, especially when tracking is done across multiple domains using third-party cookies. For this reason, some countries have legislation about cookies.
</p><p>Website operators who do not disclose third-party cookie use to consumers run the risk of harming consumer trust if cookie use is discovered. Having clear disclosure (such as in a <a href="/wiki/Privacy_policy" title="Privacy policy">privacy policy</a>) tends to eliminate any negative effects of such cookie discovery.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dCj3R&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dCj3R-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The article says the opposite: that apriori disclosure of cookies reduces trust, and that nobody reads privacy policies anyway. (October 2022)">failed verification</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> government set strict rules on setting cookies in 2000 after it was disclosed that the White House <a href="/wiki/Office_of_National_Drug_Control_Policy" title="Office of National Drug Control Policy">drug policy office</a> used cookies to track computer users viewing its online anti-drug advertising. In 2002, privacy activist Daniel Brandt found that the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">CIA</a> had been leaving persistent cookies on computers that had visited its website. When notified it was violating policy, CIA stated that these cookies were not intentionally set and stopped setting them. On 25 December 2005, Brandt discovered that the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a> (NSA) had been leaving two persistent cookies on visitors' computers due to a software upgrade. After being informed, the NSA immediately disabled the cookies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-BnfI7&#95;73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BnfI7-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="EU_cookie_directive">EU cookie directive</h3></div>
<table class="box-Primary&#95;sources plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Primary&#95;sources" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/60px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/120px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399"></a></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>relies excessively on <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">references</a> to <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research">primary sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please improve this section by adding <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research">secondary or tertiary sources</a>. <br><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22GDPR%22">"GDPR"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22GDPR%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22GDPR%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22GDPR%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22GDPR%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22GDPR%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2022</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In 2002, the European Union launched the <a href="/wiki/Directive_on_Privacy_and_Electronic_Communications" class="mw-redirect" title="Directive on Privacy and Electronic Communications">Directive on Privacy and Electronic Communications</a> (e-Privacy Directive), a policy requiring end users' consent for the placement of cookies, and similar technologies for storing and accessing information on users' equipment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-JISC&#95;74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JISC-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ICO&#95;reference&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICO_reference-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In particular, Article 5 Paragraph 3 mandates that storing technically unnecessary data on a user's computer can only be done if the user is provided information about how this data is used, and the user is given the possibility of denying this storage operation. The Directive does not require users to authorise or be provided notice of cookie usage that are functionally required for delivering a service they have requested, for example to retain settings, store log-in sessions, or remember what is in a user's shopping basket.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2009, the law was amended by Directive 2009/136/EC, which included a change to Article 5, Paragraph 3. Instead of having an option for users to opt out of cookie storage, the revised Directive requires consent to be obtained for cookie storage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ICO&#95;reference&#95;75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICO_reference-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The definition of consent is cross-referenced to the definition in European data protection law, firstly the Data Protection Directive 1995 and subsequently the <a href="/wiki/General_Data_Protection_Regulation" title="General Data Protection Regulation">General Data Protection Regulation</a> (GDPR). As the definition of consent was strengthened in the text of the GDPR, this increased the quality of consent required by those storing and accessing information such as cookies on users devices. In a case decided under the Data Protection Directive however, the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Justice_of_the_European_Union" title="Court of Justice of the European Union">Court of Justice of the European Union</a> later confirmed however that the previous law implied the same strong quality of consent as the current instrument.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-eur-lex.europa.eu&#95;77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eur-lex.europa.eu-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In addition to the requirement of consent which stems from storing or accessing information on a user's terminal device, the information in many cookies will be considered personal data under the GDPR alone, and will require a legal basis to process. This has been the case since the 1995 Data Protection Directive, which used an identical definition of personal data, although the GDPR in interpretative Recital 30 clarifies that cookie identifiers are included. While not all data processing under the GDPR requires consent, the characteristics of behavioural advertising mean that it is difficult or impossible to justify under any other ground.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Veale&#95;78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veale-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Consent under the combination of the GDPR and e-Privacy Directive has to meet a number of conditions in relation to cookies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It must be freely given and unambiguous: preticked boxes were banned under both the Data Protection Directive 1995<sup id="cite&#95;ref-eur-lex.europa.eu&#95;77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eur-lex.europa.eu-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the GDPR (Recital 32).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The GDPR is specific that consent must be as 'easy to withdraw as to give',<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> meaning that a reject-all button must be as easy to access in terms of clicks and visibility as an 'accept all' button.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It must be specific and informed, meaning that consent relates to particular purposes for the use of this data, and all organisations seeking to use this consent must be specifically named.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Justice_of_the_European_Union" title="Court of Justice of the European Union">Court of Justice of the European Union</a> has also ruled that consent must be 'efficient and timely', meaning that it must be gained before cookies are laid and data processing begins instead of afterwards.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The industry's response has been largely negative. Robert Bond of the law firm Speechly Bircham describes the effects as "far-reaching and incredibly onerous" for "all UK companies". Simon Davis of <a href="/wiki/Privacy_International" title="Privacy International">Privacy International</a> argues that proper enforcement would "destroy the entire industry".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sKaxf&#95;85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sKaxf-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, scholars note that the onerous nature of cookie pop-ups stems from an attempt to continue to operate a business model through convoluted requests that may be incompatible with the GDPR.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Veale&#95;78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veale-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Academic studies and regulators both describe widespread non-compliance with the law. A study scraping 10,000 UK websites found that only 11.8% of sites adhered to minimal legal requirements, with only 33.4% of websites studied providing a mechanism to reject cookies that was as easy to use as accepting them.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A study of 17,000 websites found that 84% of sites breached this criterion, finding additionally that many laid third party cookies with no notice at all.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The UK regulator, the <a href="/wiki/Information_Commissioner%27s_Office" title="Information Commissioner&#39;s Office">Information Commissioner's Office</a>, stated in 2019 that the industry's 'Transparency and Consent Framework' from the advertising technology group the <a href="/wiki/Interactive_Advertising_Bureau" title="Interactive Advertising Bureau">Interactive Advertising Bureau</a> was 'insufficient to ensure transparency and fair processing of the personal data in question and therefore also insufficient to provide for free and informed consent, with attendant implications for PECR [e-Privacy] compliance.'<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many companies that sell compliance solutions (Consent Management Platforms) permit them to be configured in manifestly illegal ways, which scholars have noted creates questions around the appropriate allocation of liability.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/W3C" class="mw-redirect" title="W3C">W3C</a> specification called <a href="/wiki/P3P" title="P3P">P3P</a> was proposed for servers to communicate their privacy policy to browsers, allowing automatic, user-configurable handling. However, few websites implement the specification, and the W3C has discontinued work on the specification.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>
Third-party cookies can be blocked by most browsers to increase privacy and reduce tracking by advertising and tracking companies without negatively affecting the user's web experience on all sites. Some sites operate 'cookie walls', which make access to a site conditional on allowing cookies either technically in a browser, through pressing 'accept', or both.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2020, the <a href="/wiki/European_Data_Protection_Board" title="European Data Protection Board">European Data Protection Board</a>, composed of all EU data protection regulators, stated that cookie walls were illegal.</p><blockquote><p>In order for consent to be freely given, access to services and functionalities must not be made conditional on the consent of a user to the storing of information, or gaining of access to information already stored, in the terminal equipment of a user (so called cookie walls).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Many advertising operators have an opt-out option to behavioural advertising, with a generic cookie in the browser stopping behavioural advertising.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-nyt-bits&#95;91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-bits-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-howtoblock&#95;92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-howtoblock-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, this is often ineffective against many forms of tracking, such as first-party tracking that is growing in popularity to avoid the impact of browsers blocking third party cookies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Furthermore, if such a setting is more difficult to place than the acceptance of tracking, it remains in breach of the conditions of the e-Privacy Directive.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Cookie_theft_and_session_hijacking">Cookie theft and session hijacking</h2></div>
<table class="box-Multiple&#95;issues plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-multiple&#95;issues compact-ambox" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/120px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40"></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span"><div class="multiple-issues-text mw-collapsible"><b>This section has multiple issues.</b> Please help <b><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/HTTP_cookie" title="Special:EditPage/HTTP cookie">improve it</a></b> or discuss these issues on the <b><a href="/wiki/Talk:HTTP_cookie" title="Talk:HTTP cookie">talk page</a></b>. <small><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove these messages</a>)</i></small>
<div class="mw-collapsible-content">
      <table class="box-Original&#95;research plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Original&#95;research" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/120px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40"></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>may contain <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">original research</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HTTP_cookie&amp;action=edit">improve it</a> by <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verifying</a> the claims made and adding <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Inline_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">inline citations</a>. <table class="box-More&#95;citations&#95;needed&#95;section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/60px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/120px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399"></a></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/HTTP_cookie" title="Special:EditPage/HTTP cookie">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>&#32;in this section.
